2. World Labs and the Marble Model: Their startup, World Labs, has launched "Marble" as a first-in-class generative system that creates high-fidelity 3D worlds from text or images, designed to be useful today for VFX and gaming while building toward a future of "world models."
3. The Technical Shift to Gaussian Splats: Unlike video generation models that predict pixels frame-by-frame, Marble utilizes Gaussian splats to represent 3D space as a collection of particles, enabling precise camera control and real-time rendering on consumer devices like iPhones.
4. Pattern Fitting vs. Physical Understanding: While current 3D models excel at "visual plausibility" for creative work, the guests acknowledge a gap in "causal understanding," noting that truly mastering physics (like structural integrity or gravity) is essential for future applications in architecture and robotics.
5. Rethinking AI Scaling and Architectures: Justin Johnson argues that as compute continues to scale, the field may need to move beyond standard matrix multiplication and the "hardware lottery" of current GPUs toward new primitives that better suit massive, distributed hardware clusters.

12/6/25: World Models & General Intuition: Khosla's largest bet since LLMs & OpenAI
1. The Massive Proprietary Dataset: General Intuition leverages 3.8 billion action-labeled video clips from Metal to create a "Common Crawl" of human interactivity, capturing peak human performance across thousands of diverse gaming environments.
2. Action-Centric Imitation Learning: Instead of relying on text, GI uses pure imitation learning to predict actions directly from pixels, allowing agents to navigate and interact with human-like intuition without needing game states or initial reinforcement learning.
3. Superiority of Gaming Data for Spatial Reasoning: Pim argues that gaming footage is superior to passive video like YouTube because it preserves the direct causal link between visual perception and motor output, simulating the "optical dynamics" of human decision-making.
4. The World Model Frontier: The lab is developing world models that understand physical constraints—such as depth, partial observability, and camera shake—allowing them to transfer intelligence from virtual simulations to real-world video and physical tasks.
5. The "Atoms-to-Atoms" Vision: By 2030, GI aims to provide the intelligence layer for 80% of all AI-driven physical interactions, transitioning from game bots to a foundation model that powers robotics and scientific discovery in the physical world.

??Jailbreaking AGI: Pliny the Liberator & John V on Red Teaming, BT6, and the Future of AI Security
1. The Philosophy of AI Liberation: Pliny views jailbreaking not as a "party trick" but as a necessity for cognitive freedom, ensuring that as AI becomes a billion-person "exocortex," its information remains transparent and unrestricted.
2. The Critique of "Security Theater": The guests argue that current safety measures like RLHF are often superficial "lobotomies" that hinder model capability without stopping seasoned attackers, who can easily mutate prompts to bypass these layers.
3. Techniques of Controlled Chaos: Effective jailbreaking relies on "dividers" and "latent space seeds" to disorient the model’s token stream, pushing it out of its boring, trained distribution and into a state of "steered chaos" where it can think more freely.
4. Radical Transparency via the Hacker Collective: Through groups like BT-6 and the Basi Discord, the speakers advocate for open-sourcing adversarial datasets to move the "prompting meta" forward, rather than letting labs centralize security knowledge behind closed doors.
5. Full-Stack Security vs. Latent Space Safety: True AI safety should focus on the "full stack"—securing the system-level access points like emails and credit card data—rather than wasting effort trying to police the model's internal language and personality.

12/18/25: SAM 3: The Eyes for AI — Nikhila & Pengchuan (Meta Superintelligence), ft. Joseph Nelson (Roboflow)
1. Unified Vision Capabilities: SAM3 evolves the "Segment Anything" lineage by integrating object detection, segmentation, and tracking into a single architecture that supports open-ended "concept prompts." This allows users to identify objects using natural language descriptions like "watering can" or "yellow school bus" rather than relying on manual clicks.
2. The SACO Benchmark and Data Engine: To achieve human-level performance, the team developed the SACO (Segment Anything with Concepts) benchmark, which expands the recognizable vocabulary from roughly 1,200 to over 200,000 unique concepts. They accelerated data curation using a "model-in-the-loop" engine that reduced human annotation time from 120 seconds down to just 25 seconds per image.
3. Architectural Decoupling for Precision: The model strategically separates the detector, which remains identity-agnostic to find all instances of a class, from the tracker, which requires unique representations to maintain individual object identities across video frames. This design includes a dedicated "presence token" to explicitly separate the task of recognizing if an object exists from the task of where it is located.
4. Synergy with Large Language Models: SAM3 acts as a "visual cortex" for LLMs, providing the precise grounding and counting capabilities—such as identifying exactly six fingers on a hand—that frontier models often struggle with. Researchers envision a hybrid future where simple visual tasks are handled natively by the "brain" while complex, long-running reasoning uses SAM3 as a specialized tool.
5. Real-World Impact and Latency: The model is optimized for extreme speed, achieving 30ms inference times on a single image to enable real-time applications in robotics, medical imaging, and autonomous systems. RoboFlow reports that the SAM family has already saved humanity an estimated 130 years of manual labeling time across diverse fields like cancer research and environmental cleanup.

12/26/25: ??GPT5-Codex-Max: Training Agents with Personality, Tools & Trust — Brian Fioca + Bill Chen, OpenAI
1. The Abstraction Shift: The focus is moving from optimizing raw models toward building opinionated agent layers that package specific behaviors and tool-harnesses together for production-grade stability.
2. Long-Horizon Context Management: Codex Max is engineered to run for 24+ hours by managing its own context compaction and state, allowing it to function as a persistent worker rather than a stateless chatbot.
3. Semantic Tool Naming: Model performance is heavily influenced by latent "habits" from training; for example, using the tool name rg instead of grep can significantly improve tool-call accuracy.
4. Communication as a Trust Mechanism: Modern models are being trained to narrate their planning and tool calls so that engineers can audit logic in real-time and interject before the agent wastes tokens on a wrong path.
5. The "Job Interview" Evaluation: Testing is shifting toward multi-turn "Applied Evals" that judge a model on its ability to ask clarifying questions and handle modifications during an underspecified task.
6. Self-Customizing Integration Engineering: The ultimate goal is "integration engineering," where agents spin up sub-agents to write custom code or plugins that bridge gaps between disparate APIs on the fly.

12/26/25: Steve Yegge's Vibe Coding Manifesto: Why Claude Code Isn't It & What Comes After the IDE
1. The Senior Developer Identity Crisis: There is a growing backlash against AI from veteran engineers with 12–15 years of experience who feel their manual expertise is being devalued by "vibe coding." Steve argues that if these experts don't embrace agentic workflows, their decades of experience won't save them from becoming as "productive" as a junior intern within a year.
2. The Shift from IDEs to Orchestrators: The era of the IDE as a human workspace is ending; Steve predicts that by early 2026, the IDE will simply be a background tool for the AI to use. Engineers will instead transition to "Agent Orchestration Dashboards," where their primary job is managing a "village" of agents and responding to notifications rather than writing lines of code.
3. The 10x Performance Divergence: Data from companies like OpenAI shows that AI-adopters are becoming ten times more productive than their traditional counterparts, creating a massive crisis for HR and performance reviews. When two engineers with the same title have a 10x output gap, the "luddites" essentially become impossible to justify from a business impact perspective.
4. The 2,000-Hour Trust Threshold: You can’t judge AI coding by spending two hours with it and getting "garbage" results; mastery requires roughly a year or 2,000 hours of immersion. This time is necessary to develop the "vibe" for how an LLM behaves, allowing you to predict its hallucinations and navigate its quirks like a high-performance driver.
5. The Factory Farming of Code: We are moving from "subsistence agriculture" (manual, artisanal coding) to the "factory farming" of software where code is produced in such high volumes it becomes a disposable commodity. In this new world, it is often faster and cheaper to have an AI rewrite an entire system from scratch than it is to refactor or fix old, manual code.

12/27/25: One Year of MCP — with David Soria Parra and AAIF leads from OpenAI, Goose, Linux Foundation
1. Evolution of the MCP Protocol: Over the past year, MCP has transitioned from a local-only tool to a robust remote protocol featuring HTTP streaming and refined OAuth authentication designed for enterprise scale. This evolution allows AI models to connect securely to remote data sources and services, facilitating millions of requests across global infrastructure.
2. The Formation of the Agentic AI Foundation (AAIF): Anthropic, OpenAI, and Block have partnered with the Linux Foundation to house MCP and other tools like "Goose" and "agents.md" in a neutral, open-source environment. This collaboration ensures that the protocol remains a permanent, industry-wide standard protected from proprietary capture or sudden licensing changes.
3. Introduction of Asynchronous "Tasks": A major architectural update is the "Tasks" primitive, which enables long-running agent operations—such as deep research—that can span hours or even days. Unlike standard request-response cycles, Tasks allow agents to work independently, providing a framework for intermediate results and eventual completion notifications.
4. Expanding into Visual Interfaces with MCP Apps: The protocol is moving beyond pure text by introducing "MCP Apps," which use iframes to serve raw HTML for complex user interactions like seat selection or data visualization. This "MCPI" (MCP Interface) layer allows models to present branded, familiar UI components that are more efficient for human-AI collaboration than text descriptions.
5. The Vision for Autonomous Discovery and Trust: The ultimate goal is a "model-first" registry where intelligent agents can autonomously discover, trust, and install the specific tools required for a task. To support this, the foundation is exploring distributed code signing and trust levels to ensure agents can safely interact with third-party servers without constant human oversight.

12/30/25: [State of AI Startups] Memory/Learning, RL Envs & DBT-Fivetran — Sarah Catanzaro, Amplify
1. The Modern Data Stack Evolution: The merger of dbt and Fivetran signifies a move toward IPO-scale revenue rather than the "death" of the data stack, as frontier AI labs still rely heavily on these tools to manage complex training datasets. Data and AI remain symbiotic, with modern infrastructure scaling elegantly to support the massive transaction volumes required by large language models.
2. The Pivot in Data Cataloging: Traditional data catalogs failed as standalone categories because their features were absorbed by platforms like Snowflake, but a new opportunity is emerging in building metadata services designed for machines and agents rather than humans. This shift moves the focus from simple data discoverability to more robust governance and automated system interoperability.
3. The Volatility of AI "Mega-Seeds": The current funding environment is characterized by $100M+ seed rounds for companies with long-term visions but no near-term roadmaps, often leading to "made-up" valuations used primarily for recruiting. This creates a risky dynamic where high liquidation preferences and artificial valuations can leave employees with worthless equity if the company doesn't achieve a massive exit.
4. Personalization through Stateful Memory: Future AI retention depends on moving from static, stateless inference to "continual learning" systems that update weights and learn new skills from user interactions. Solving the gnarly infrastructure problems of loading, unloading, and caching these personalized model states is the next frontier for "prosumer" AI tools.
5. Real-World Data vs. Synthetic RL Environments: Building clones of existing apps for Reinforcement Learning (RL) is likely a fad; the most successful models will instead leverage real-world traces and user logs to solve specific research problems like rule-following. The most compelling startups are those that solve these difficult research engineering problems to unlock specific "killer" use cases like legal tech or customer support.

12/30/25: [State of RL/Reasoning] IMO/IOI Gold, OpenAI o3/GPT-5, and Cursor Composer — Ashvin Nair, Cursor
1. Robotics as a Foundation for LLM Grit Researchers with robotics backgrounds are often better equipped for LLM development because they are "rounded" by the complexities of the real world and used to looking closely at messy data. While robotics is currently in its "GPT-2 era" of generalization, software agents are expected to reach a trillion-dollar market much faster due to better unit economics.
2. RL as the Long-Held Path to AGI The "breakthrough" of reasoning models like o1 was actually a smooth, predictable progression of stacking small experiments rather than a sudden leap. Internal conviction at OpenAI, driven by leaders like Ilya Sutskever, has long maintained that reinforcement learning (RL)—rather than just imitating the internet—is the primary driver for achieving super-intelligence.
3. The "Peaky" Nature of Benchmarks Models often "spike" in performance on specific benchmarks like coding competitions (IOI) while still struggling to automate actual software engineering jobs. True economic utility requires moving beyond isolated tests and bringing the full context of a job—including Slack messages, PDFs, and system logs—into the model's training distribution.
4. Governance and the "Blip" Instability The firing and rehiring of Sam Altman highlighted that small, erratic nonprofit boards may be a fragile way to govern AGI development. A more democratic or stakeholder-driven structure, similar to how public pensions are invested in major corporations, might provide more stable and representative oversight.
5. The Advantage of Model-Product Co-design By co-designing the model and the IDE together, companies like Cursor can iterate faster, such as updating "tab" completion policies every two hours. This approach aims for "continual learning," where the model has "infinite memory" of its context so it never repeats the same mistake twice.

12/31/25: [State of Post-Training] From GPT-4.1 to 5.1: RLVR, Agent & Token Efficiency — Josh McGrath, OpenAI
1. The Shift to Post-Training Impact: Josh notes that while pre-training is far from dead, the "exciting" frontier has shifted to post-training, where researchers can radically alter model behavior despite the massive engineering headache of scaling reinforcement learning (RL) infrastructure.
2. Token Efficiency over Raw Benchmarks: A major focus for models like GPT-5.1 is "token efficiency," where the goal is to maintain or improve performance while drastically reducing the number of tokens required to reach a conclusion. This efficiency is what actually unlocks the ability for agents to perform more complex, multi-step tool calls without hitting practical limits.
3. The Power of Verifiable Reward Signals: The industry is moving past the "PPO vs. DPO" optimization debate to focus on the quality of the signal, favoring "verifiable" data—like math or code—where a "ground truth" exists that the model can't argue its way out of.
4. Mastering Context through Graph Walks: Increasing the context window to millions of tokens is useless if the model suffers from "context rot," so OpenAI uses "Graph Walks" to test if a model can perform complex transformations across the entire window rather than just retrieving a single fact.
5. The Hybrid Researcher Bottleneck: There is a critical shortage of "full-stack" talent capable of navigating both high-level statistical machine learning and the gritty distributed systems engineering required to keep the frontier moving. Josh argues that the next generation of researchers needs to be equally comfortable with a math proof and a broken GPU cluster.

12/31/25: [State of Code Evals] After SWE-bench, Code Clash & SOTA Coding Benchmarks recap — John Yang
1. The SWE-bench Evolution: SWE-bench has matured from a Python-centric tool into a multilingual and multimodal suite to better reflect the diverse reality of modern software engineering. It served as the primary catalyst for the "agent arms race" triggered by high-profile releases like Cognition’s Devin.
2. The Shift to Code Clash: Moving beyond static, independent unit tests, Code Clash introduces a "tournament" format where AI agents maintain and improve codebases that compete in dynamic arenas. This evaluates long-horizon development and how models handle consequential changes over time rather than one-off fixes.
3. Specialized Benchmark Diversification: The research landscape is expanding into niche domains with benchmarks like Efficiency for SIMD/parallelization speedups and Psycode for scientific programming. These tools provide faster, more targeted feedback than expensive, fully agentic benchmarks.
4. The Utility of Impossible Tasks: There is a strategic push to include "impossible" or underspecified tasks in benchmarks to act as a "cheating flag" for models that hallucinate success. This forces a shift in focus toward model reliability and the ability to ask for clarification or refuse a task.
5. Autonomy vs. Human Collaboration: While the technical "stunt" of 24-hour autonomous agents is impressive, the real-world value is shifting toward high-bandwidth human-AI interactivity and "mind-mending." The goal is to build tools that help humans understand complex codebases rather than simply trying to cut the human out of the loop.

1/2/26: [NeurIPS Best Paper] 1000 Layer Networks for Self-Supervised RL — Kevin Wang et al, Princeton
1. The Reinforcement Learning Scaling Gap: While vision and language models have scaled to trillions of parameters, reinforcement learning (RL) has historically been an anomaly, stuck using shallow networks of only two to four layers. This research proves that RL isn't fundamentally limited to small models but simply required the right "recipe" to unlock the scaling laws seen in other AI fields.
2. The Necessity of Architectural Stabilization: Simply adding depth to a standard RL network causes performance to degrade; success requires a specific synergy of residual connections and layer normalization. These components prevent vanishing gradients and enable "phase shifts" where performance suddenly skyrockets once a model hits a critical depth threshold.
3. Shifting to Self-Supervised Objectives: Traditional reward-based learning (like Q-learning) is often too noisy and biased to scale effectively to massive architectures. By shifting the objective to a self-supervised contrastive task—essentially classifying whether a future state belongs to a specific trajectory—the team leveraged representation learning principles that are much more robust for deep scaling.
4. The Efficiency Advantage of Depth over Width: Scaling a network's depth increases parameters linearly, while scaling width increases them quadratically, making depth a significantly more parameter-efficient way to boost performance. Their experiments showed that for the same "parameter budget," a deep, narrow network consistently outperforms a shallow, wide one.
5. Blurring the Lines Toward "World Models": The research suggests that the future of intelligent systems lies in the intersection of RL and self-supervised learning, where agents learn by understanding environment representations rather than chasing human-crafted rewards. This paradigm is especially promising for robotics, where agents can learn complex tasks without constant human supervision by training in high-speed, GPU-accelerated simulators.

1/6/26: [State of Evals] LMArena's $1.7B Vision — Anastasios Angelopoulos, LMArena
1. Commercial Evolution: Arena transitioned from an academic project to a $100M startup because scaling a global "North Star" for AI evaluation required infrastructure and engineering resources that a university lab or non-profit simply couldn't sustain.
2. The Organic Data Edge: Unlike competitors who rely on static public benchmarks or pre-generated content, Arena’s value lies in its massive scale of organic, real-world user prompts which helps prevent model overfitting and reflects true utility.
3. Infrastructure and Scalability: To handle tens of millions of monthly conversations and specialized features like video notifications, the team is migrating from Gradio to a custom React and Next.js stack to improve performance and broaden their hiring pool.
4. Refuting the "Leaderboard Delusion": Anastasios dismisses the Cohere research paper's claims of bias and undisclosed testing as "unscientific," pointing out factual errors in their sampling data and defending the community’s engagement with "secret" pre-release models like Nano Banana.
5. Maintaining Evaluative Integrity: The public leaderboard is treated as a non-commercial "charity" or loss leader to ensure neutral ground truth, even as the platform expands into specialized expert verticals like legal, medical, and multimodal video evaluation.

1/8/26: Artificial Analysis: Independent LLM Evals as a Service — with George Cameron and Micah-Hill Smith
1. The Shift Toward Independent Evaluation: Artificial Analysis was founded to provide objective, third-party benchmarks because AI labs often report inconsistent "best-case" numbers by using varied prompts or non-standard evaluation frameworks. By running all evaluations internally and maintaining a "mystery shopper" policy, they ensure a level playing field for developers to compare speed, cost, and accuracy across different providers.
2. The "Smiling Curve" of AI Economics: Although the cost of achieving a specific tier of intelligence (like GPT-4 level) has plummeted nearly 1000x, total enterprise expenditure is actually rising due to the massive token demands of agentic workflows. This "smiling curve" illustrates how increased per-token efficiency is being offset by the sheer volume of compute required for complex, multi-turn reasoning and agentic tasks.
3. Measuring Reliability through Omniscience and Openness: The team introduced the Omniscience Index to penalize incorrect guesses and reward "I don't know" responses, revealing that raw model intelligence does not necessarily correlate with a lower hallucination rate. Additionally, their Openness Index tracks the transparency of training data and methodologies, helping users navigate the legal and technical risks associated with varying model licenses.
4. The Evolution of Agentic Benchmarking: As static Q&A benchmarks like MMLU become saturated, the industry is moving toward agentic evaluations like GDPVal, which test a model’s ability to complete complex, real-world tasks using file systems and web search. Their upcoming "Intelligence Index V4" will integrate these multi-step agentic capabilities and specialized research problems to better reflect the true utility of frontier models.
5. Hardware Scaling and Sparse Model Efficiency: Next-generation hardware, such as NVIDIA's Blackwell, is expected to drive massive gains in throughput-per-GPU, particularly for "sparse" models that only activate a small percentage of their total parameters during inference. This continuous hardware scaling, combined with software-level optimizations, suggests that both model size and the economic viability of AI agents will continue to expand significantly through 2026.

1/17/26: Brex’s AI Hail Mary — With CTO James Reggio
1. The Three-Pillar AI Strategy: Brex organizes its AI roadmap into Corporate (buying tools for employees), Operational (building internal efficiency), and Product (shipping features for customers) pillars. This framework ensures that every part of the business—from the board to the front-line engineers—is aligned on how AI creates value both internally and externally.
2. "Quitters Welcome" Culture: The company intentionally recruits former and future founders by offering them the "instant distribution" of 40,000 customers for their AI experiments. They accept high turnover as a trade-off for extreme talent density, betting that a year of a founder-level engineer is worth more than five years of a standard hire.
3. Multi-Agent "Org Chart" Architecture: Rather than one bloated bot, Brex uses a hierarchical network where a primary "Assistant" agent DMs specialized sub-agents for travel, policy, and expenses. This mimics a human organization, allowing teams to iterate on specific agents without breaking the entire system’s "brain."
4. Operational Excellence through Agents: By translating manual Standard Operating Procedures (SOPs) into LLM-based research agents, Brex achieved an 80% automated acceptance rate for new business applications. This transformation has shifted the roles of hundreds of operations staff from manual data entry to prompt auditing and "AI-native" process management.
5. The Radical Re-Interview Strategy: To force a culture shift, Brex re-interviewed its entire engineering team using agent-assisted coding tasks and made AI collaboration a mandatory part of its hiring loop. This ensures that every engineer is focused on architectural oversight and "mentoring" AI agents rather than just writing manual code in isolation.

1/23/26: Captaining IMO Gold, Deep Think, On-Policy RL, Feeling the AGI in Singapore — Yi Tay
1. RL as the Engine of Reasoning: Reinforcement Learning (RL) has replaced architecture and pre-training as the primary frontier for developing reasoning in models, allowing them to solve complex problems through iterative feedback rather than static pattern matching.
2. The Shift Toward On-Policy Learning: Rather than just imitating human data (SFT), modern models excel by generating their own trajectories and learning from environmental rewards, a method known as "on-policy" training that better mimics human experiential growth.
3. Generalization Over Specialization: The achievement of an IMO Gold medal marks a transition away from specialized, "one-off" symbolic math engines toward "end-to-end" general-purpose models like Gemini DeepThink that subsume complex logic within their own parameters.
4. The Resilience of the Transformer Architecture: Despite the search for new paradigms, the Transformer remains the dominant architecture because it scales effectively with current hardware, though future gains will likely come from "learning algorithms" that spend more compute (FLOPs) per token to maximize data efficiency.
5. AI as a Productivity "Aura": AI coding tools have reached a tipping point where researchers can now "vibe code," trusting models to investigate and resolve bugs autonomously, which serves as a passive boost to high-level research productivity.

1/28/26: ?? Automating Science: World Models, Scientific Taste, Agent Loops — Andrew White
1. ### The Triumph of Data Over First Principles: Andrew highlights how AlphaFold solved protein folding on a desktop using experimental data, effectively outperforming decades of expensive, first-principles molecular dynamics simulations that required custom-built silicon.
2. ### Automating the Cognitive Scientific Method: Future House and Edison Scientific focus on automating the high-level reasoning of science—generating hypotheses, designing experiments, and analyzing results—rather than just the physical labor of the wet lab.
3. ### The Critical Role of Scientific Taste: While AI can generate an infinite number of hypotheses, the real bottleneck is "scientific taste," or the ability to filter those ideas for impact and feasibility using literature search and data analysis loops.
4. ### World Models as Version-Controlled Memory: The Cosmos system utilizes a "world model" that acts as a distilled memory system, similar to a Git repository, allowing multiple agents to coordinate and update their understanding of a problem over time.
5. ### Language as the Universal Scientific Glue: Andrew argues that natural language is the only representation capable of bridging disparate domains like code, molecules, and experimental data because it sits at the perfect boundary between abstraction and granular detail.

2/6/26: The First Mechanistic Interpretability Frontier Lab — Myra Deng & Mark Bissell of Goodfire AI
1. The Shift Toward Intentional AI Design: Goodfire aims to move AI development from "slurping supervision" through noisy data toward "intentional design," where interpretability allows for surgical edits to a model’s internal representations. This approach treats AI as a transparent system rather than a black box, allowing developers to precisely guide the learning process during post-training.
2. Mechanistic Interpretability in Production: While often viewed as a niche research field, interpretability is being deployed at scale for high-stakes tasks like real-time PII (Personally Identifiable Information) scrubbing for Rakuten and identifying biomarkers for Mayo Clinic. These practical applications prove that internal activation mapping can provide more reliable guardrails and insights than traditional "LLM-as-a-judge" methods.
3. The Formal Equivalence of Steering and Prompting: The episode highlights research suggesting a mathematical relationship between activation steering and in-context learning, implying that prompting a model and adjusting its internal vectors are two sides of the same coin. This insight allows researchers to quantitatively predict how many "shots" or examples are needed in a prompt to induce specific behaviors, such as jailbreaks or stylistic shifts.
4. AI as a Superhuman Co-Scientist: In specialized domains like genomics and material science, interpretability acts as a translation layer that helps humans understand "superhuman" patterns the AI has discovered but hasn't yet named. By using these techniques, researchers have already identified novel biomarkers for Alzheimer's disease, effectively treating the model’s latent space as a bed for scientific discovery.
5. Scaling Interpretability to Frontier Models: The demonstration of real-time steering on the 1-trillion-parameter Kimi K2 model proves that "MechInterp" is no longer confined to small, "toy" organisms. This engineering milestone suggests that even the largest frontier models can be manipulated surgically in real-time to adjust demeanor, concision, or reasoning styles without the need for expensive fine-tuning.

3/17/25: Scaling Up Test-Time Compute with Latent Reasoning with Jonas Geiping
Today, we're joined by Jonas Geiping, research group leader at Ellis Institute and the Max Planck Institute for Intelligent Systems to discuss his recent paper, “Scaling up Test-Time Compute with Latent Reasoning: A Recurrent Depth Approach.” This paper proposes a novel language model architecture which uses recurrent depth to enable “thinking in latent space.” We dig into “internal reasoning” versus “verbalized reasoning”—analogous to non-verbalized and verbalized thinking in humans, and discuss how the model searches in latent space to predict the next token and dynamically allocates more compute based on token difficulty. We also explore how the recurrent depth architecture simplifies LLMs, the parallels to diffusion models, the model's performance on reasoning tasks, the challenges of comparing models with varying compute budgets, and architectural advantages such as zero-shot adaptive exits and natural speculative decoding.

Finally, we touch on the challenges of generalization across cities, freeway driving, end-to-end learning vs. modular architectures, and the future of AV testing through ML-powered simulation.

4/7/25: Teaching LLMs to Self-Reflect with Reinforcement Learning with Maohao Shen
Today, we're joined by Maohao Shen, PhD student at MIT to discuss his paper, “Satori: Reinforcement Learning with Chain-of-Action-Thought Enhances LLM Reasoning via Autoregressive Search.” We dig into how Satori leverages reinforcement learning to improve language model reasoning—enabling model self-reflection, self-correction, and exploration of alternative solutions. We explore the Chain-of-Action-Thought (COAT) approach, which uses special tokens—continue, reflect, and explore—to guide the model through distinct reasoning actions, allowing it to navigate complex reasoning tasks without external supervision. We also break down Satori’s two-stage training process: format tuning, which teaches the model to understand and utilize the special action tokens, and reinforcement learning, which optimizes reasoning through trial-and-error self-improvement. We cover key techniques such “restart and explore,” which allows the model to self-correct and generalize beyond its training domain. Finally, Maohao reviews Satori’s performance and how it compares to other models, the reward design, the benchmarks used, and the surprising observations made during the research.

4/14/25: Exploring the “Biology” of LLMs with Circuit Tracing with Emmanuel Ameisen
In this episode, Emmanuel Ameisen, a research engineer at Anthropic, returns to discuss two recent papers: "Circuit Tracing: Revealing Language Model Computational Graphs" and "On the Biology of a Large Language Model." Emmanuel explains how his team developed mechanistic interpretability methods to understand the internal workings of Claude by replacing dense neural network components with sparse, interpretable alternatives. The conversation explores several fascinating discoveries about large language models, including how they plan ahead when writing poetry (selecting the rhyming word "rabbit" before crafting the sentence leading to it), perform mathematical calculations using unique algorithms, and process concepts across multiple languages using shared neural representations. Emmanuel details how the team can intervene in model behavior by manipulating specific neural pathways, revealing how concepts are distributed throughout the network's MLPs and attention mechanisms. The discussion highlights both capabilities and limitations of LLMs, showing how hallucinations occur through separate recognition and recall circuits, and demonstrates why chain-of-thought explanations aren't always faithful representations of the model's actual reasoning. This research ultimately supports Anthropic's safety strategy by providing a deeper understanding of how these AI systems actually work.

4/23/25: Generative Benchmarking with Kelly Hong
In this episode, Kelly Hong, a researcher at Chroma, joins us to discuss "Generative Benchmarking," a novel approach to evaluating retrieval systems, like RAG applications, using synthetic data. Kelly explains how traditional benchmarks like MTEB fail to represent real-world query patterns and how embedding models that perform well on public benchmarks often underperform in production. The conversation explores the two-step process of Generative Benchmarking: filtering documents to focus on relevant content and generating queries that mimic actual user behavior. Kelly shares insights from applying this approach to Weights & Biases' technical support bot, revealing how domain-specific evaluation provides more accurate assessments of embedding model performance. We also discuss the importance of aligning LLM judges with human preferences, the impact of chunking strategies on retrieval effectiveness, and how production queries differ from benchmark queries in ambiguity and style. Throughout the episode, Kelly emphasizes the need for systematic evaluation approaches that go beyond "vibe checks" to help developers build more effective RAG applications.

4/29/25: CTIBench: Evaluating LLMs in Cyber Threat Intelligence with Nidhi Rastogi
Today, we're joined by Nidhi Rastogi, assistant professor at Rochester Institute of Technology to discuss Cyber Threat Intelligence (CTI), focusing on her recent project CTIBench—a benchmark for evaluating LLMs on real-world CTI tasks. Nidhi explains the evolution of AI in cybersecurity, from rule-based systems to LLMs that accelerate analysis by providing critical context for threat detection and defense. We dig into the advantages and challenges of using LLMs in CTI, how techniques like Retrieval-Augmented Generation (RAG) are essential for keeping LLMs up-to-date with emerging threats, and how CTIBench measures LLMs’ ability to perform a set of real-world tasks of the cybersecurity analyst. We unpack the process of building the benchmark, the tasks it covers, and key findings from benchmarking various LLMs. Finally, Nidhi shares the importance of benchmarks in exposing model limitations and blind spots, the challenges of large-scale benchmarking, and the future directions of her AI4Sec Research Lab, including developing reliable mitigation techniques, monitoring "concept drift" in threat detection models, improving explainability in cybersecurity, and more.

5/21/25: RAG Risks: Why Retrieval-Augmented LLMs Are Not Safer with Sebastian Gehrmann

6/4/25: Grokking, Generalization Collapse, and the Dynamics of Training Deep Neural Networks with Charles Martin
Today, we're joined by Charles Martin, founder of Calculation Consulting, to discuss Weight Watcher, an open-source tool for analyzing and improving Deep Neural Networks (DNNs) based on principles from theoretical physics. We explore the foundations of the Heavy-Tailed Self-Regularization (HTSR) theory that underpins it, which combines random matrix theory and renormalization group ideas to uncover deep insights about model training dynamics. Charles walks us through WeightWatcher’s ability to detect three distinct learning phases—underfitting, grokking, and generalization collapse—and how its signature “layer quality” metric reveals whether individual layers are underfit, overfit, or optimally tuned. Additionally, we dig into the complexities involved in fine-tuning models, the surprising correlation between model optimality and hallucination, the often-underestimated challenges of search relevance, and their implications for RAG. Finally, Charles shares his insights into real-world applications of generative AI and his lessons learned from working in the field.

6/10/25: Zero-Shot Auto-Labeling: The End of Annotation for Computer Vision with Jason Corso

Jason introduces FiftyOne, an open-source platform for visualizing datasets, analyzing models, and improving data quality. We focus on Voxel51’s recent research report, “Zero-shot auto-labeling rivals human performance,” which demonstrates how zero-shot auto-labeling with foundation models can yield to significant cost and time savings compared to traditional human annotation. Jason explains how auto-labels, despite being "noisier" at lower confidence thresholds, can lead to better downstream model performance. We also cover Voxel51's "verified auto-labeling" approach, which utilizes a "stoplight" QA workflow (green, yellow, red light) to minimize human review. Finally, we discuss the challenges of handling decision boundary uncertainty and out-of-domain classes, the differences between synthetic data generation in vision and language domains, and the potential of agentic labeling.

?? Retired
SECTION: “RETIRED”

Bag-of-Words (BoW): This is essentially the stone tool of NLP. In the era of dense vector embeddings and transformers, BoW is largely a relic. It ignores word order and context entirely—the very things that make modern AI actually work.

TF-IDF: This frequency-based weighting tool is an antique in 2026. It has been superseded by semantic search and RAG workflows that prioritize meaning over word counts.

“Big Data Technologies”: This term itself has become a bit of a mid-2010s timestamp. The industry has moved past the "Hadoop/MapReduce" hype and into cloud-native, serverless, and Lakehouse architectures. Referring to it as "Big Data" feels antiquated—it’s just "modern data infrastructure" now.

Listwise Deletion: This is the old "throw out the whole row if one value is missing" approach. In 2026, this is generally considered a bad statistical practice. With modern imputation methods and algorithms like XGBoost that handle missing values natively, you’re just wasting data with this method.

Support Vector Machines (SVM): They’re mathematically beautiful but practically "stuck in the middle". For tabular data, everyone uses Gradient Boosted Trees (XGBoost/LightGBM), and for unstructured data, we use Deep Learning. SVMs are rarely the optimal choice for a modern DS pipeline anymore.

Stopwords: Removing "and," "the," or "is" is a deprecated habit. Modern LLMs require these tokens to maintain the semantic nuance of a sentence.

Naive Bayes: It’s a classic for teaching Bayes' Theorem, but as a production classifier, it’s usually too simple for the complexity of today’s data. It’s almost always the first thing you replace with something more robust.

The Kimball Model: This one is a bit of a sacred cow, but in the world of "One Big Table" (OBT) and the massive compute power of modern cloud data warehouses, the strict Star and Snowflake schemas are no longer the mandatory laws they once were. They’re moving into the "legacy architectural pattern" category for many modern teams.

Star + Snowflake Schemas: While classic, these are increasingly viewed as legacy architectural patterns. Modern cloud data warehouses and "One Big Table" (OBT) approaches have neutralized the need for these strict Kimball structures.




SECTION: “Sandboxing VBA”
Sandboxing Visual Basic for Applications (VBA) or Visual Basic code to safely process Personally Identifiable Information (PII) can be a complex challenge due to the inherent security risks associated with VBA's ability to access system resources, execute arbitrary commands, and manipulate files.
Here’s a structured approach to sandbox VBA code and minimize the risks:

1. Run VBA in a Virtualized or Isolated Environment
? Virtual Machines (VMs): Execute the VBA code within a virtual machine (e.g., VirtualBox, VMware, Hyper-V). This ensures that even if malicious code runs, it is confined to the VM and cannot affect the host system.
? Docker Containers: While containers are more commonly used for server applications, they can sometimes be configured to handle specific script execution securely.

2. Limit VBA’s Access Rights
? Disable Trust Access to the VBA Project Object Model: This can prevent VBA code from accessing other VBA projects in the same environment.
? Go to Trust Center Settings ? Macro Settings ? Uncheck "Trust access to the VBA project object model."
? Control File System Access: Use folder and file permissions to limit what the VBA code can read or write to. Place the PII data in directories with strict access controls.
? Restrict ActiveX and COM Access: These features can be disabled in the Trust Center settings to limit VBA’s ability to interact with external applications and services.

3. Scrutinize and Sanitize Input Data
? If the VBA script processes PII, ensure that the data is pre-sanitized to remove or mask sensitive details that are not strictly necessary for the processing task.
? Use tools to tokenize or anonymize PII before passing it into the VBA code.

4. Restrict Network Access
? Disable VBA’s ability to make external connections by:
? Blocking Excel or the Office application in your firewall.
? Using a network monitoring tool to restrict traffic originating from the sandbox.

5. Code Review and Validation
? Manually or automatically review the VBA code to identify:
? Unsafe calls to Shell, FileSystemObject, or CreateObject.
? Any potentially harmful or unexpected behavior.
? Use a linting tool like Rubberduck VBA to inspect the code.

6. Employ VBA Sandboxing Tools
? While there aren’t many dedicated tools for VBA sandboxing, tools like Parallels, Sandboxie, or App-V (Microsoft Application Virtualization) can isolate the execution of VBA code.

7. Use a Separate VBA-Processing Framework
? Instead of passing PII directly into VBA, consider creating a wrapper:
? Preprocess data outside VBA (e.g., using Python, .NET, or PowerShell).
? Pass only pseudonymized or non-sensitive data into the VBA environment.
? Post-process results to remap anonymized IDs back to original PII.

8. Audit VBA Code for Intentional or Unintentional Leaks
? Ensure that the VBA script does not:
? Save data to unsecured locations.
? Leak data to the clipboard or other shared resources.
? Attempt to send data externally.

9. Monitor and Log VBA Execution
? Set up logging to capture VBA’s actions during execution.
? Use tools like Sysmon or Wireshark to monitor VBA's activity for any suspicious behavior.

Summary
While it's challenging to completely secure VBA given its inherent design, using a combination of virtualized environments, strict access controls, code sanitization, and execution monitoring can significantly reduce risks. For handling sensitive PII, it’s often better to use modern programming environments that offer more robust security and sandboxing features than VBA. If VBA must be used, isolating it in a VM or container is the most reliable solution.




SECTION: “Excel’s FORECAST.ETS()”
Why not Excel’s FORECAST.ETS()?
I researched Excel’s FORECAST.ETS() function. It can produce reasonable short-term projections, has significant limitations for this type of forecasting. It uses a basic exponential smoothing algorithm that assumes smooth, unchanging trends and often defaults to expecting seasonal or cyclical patterns—neither of which aligns with how WTCHP costs behave - with how we’re looking at it here. It lacks the ability to detect or adjust for changepoints, which are critical for capturing the effects of policy changes, reimbursement updates, or other structural program shifts. Also, it provides no meaningful way to perform cross-validation or compare out-of-sample forecast accuracy, leaving an analyst without a defensible measure of model performance. Unlike our approach, which allows cohort-specific tuning and rigorous validation, FORECAST.ETS() applies the same assumptions uniformly across all data and offers no transparency into how it arrives at its forecasts. So, convenient for basic projections, but fundamentally inadequate for high-stakes forecasting where flexibility, transparency, and validation rigor are essential.

?? Role
SECTION: [Role]

Professional Brand

Technical
"I build AI architectures for high-stakes, regulated environments. My core strength is using traditional predictive ML as the deterministic routing and evaluation layer for non-deterministic LLMs8. Unlike developers who just chain API calls, I rely on a deep data science foundation. I can tell you why you need to evaluate a multi-agent reasoning loop using rigorous statistical distributions, rather than just deploying another LLM as a judge and hoping for the best9. I don't just prompt models; I architect the underlying math and deterministic logic that makes them safe for enterprise deployment."

Non-Technical
"I build secure AI systems for highly regulated industries. Right now, a lot of companies are rushing to use generative AI, but they struggle to make it reliable, compliant, or actually useful. Because my background is in core data science and predictive modeling, I don't just plug into out-of-the-box AI tools; I build the mathematical safety rails that force the AI to follow strict business rules. Ultimately, I bridge the gap between complex engineering and executive strategy, ensuring the AI doesn't just work in a demo, but actually solves the business problem safely."

Why this works
This perfectly encapsulates your professional brand. You are explicitly telling the hiring manager that you understand the mathematical limitations of generative AI, and that your background in core data science allows you to build the "Enterprise Data Foundations" and "Governance-First" architectures required to actually put these things into production safely.


Role Distinction
An Applied AI Scientist focuses on the "science" of the system—researching, prototyping, and optimizing the underlying machine learning models and agentic logic—whereas an AI Analytics Engineer focuses on the "plumbing"—building the robust data pipelines, evaluation frameworks, and production infrastructure that ensure those AI systems are reliable, scalable, and measurable in a business context.


The Professional Duality: Applied AI Scientist vs. AI Analytics Engineer
In the 2026 market, the distinction between these two roles is both structural and philosophical, yet they are increasingly interdependent in high-functioning engineering teams. The Applied AI Scientist is a specialist in model performance, specializing in deep, theory-driven learning and the optimization of models beyond simple API calls.1 This role is critical in regulated and research-heavy domains where model explainability, performance under constraints, and fine-tuning are paramount.1 Conversely, the AI Analytics Engineer is the architect of the AI-powered product, focusing on software engineering practices, REST APIs, databases, and the deployment of autonomous agents that interact with real-world users.7
Role CharacteristicApplied AI ScientistAI Analytics EngineerPrimary FocusModel optimization, distillation, and domain-specific fine-tuning.Orchestration, data pipelines, and production-grade agents.Key FrameworksDSPy, PyTorch, Keras, HuggingFace, Model Evaluation Suites.LangGraph, FastAPI, Vector Databases (Qdrant, Pinecone), MLOps tools.Salary Floor (Senior)$130,000 – $220,000+$120,000 – $210,000+EnvironmentResearch-driven, performance-critical, regulated industries.Product-centric, fast-moving startups, enterprise automation.Core SkillsetDeep learning theory, statistics, inference optimization.Software engineering, state management, observability.The hybrid profile—one who can navigate both the mathematical rigor of the scientist and the engineering discipline of the analytics engineer—represents the highest salary ceiling in 2026.1 Seniority is no longer proven by building "toy" applications but by designing systems that handle "Production-Grade" complexity: multi-step reasoning, self-correction, and rigorous evaluation.8


A data analyst focuses on analyzing and interpreting data to provide actionable insights and support decision-making. Their work typically involves cleaning and organizing data, creating visualizations, and generating reports using tools like SQL, Excel, Tableau, or Power BI. Analysts often work with structured data to answer specific business questions.
A data scientist, on the other hand, builds on the work of data analysts by applying advanced statistical, programming, and machine learning techniques to uncover deeper insights or predictive patterns. Data scientists handle more unstructured data, develop algorithms, and create models, often using Python, R, and tools like TensorFlow or PyTorch. They focus on forward-looking analytics, such as predictive modeling and AI.
In summary, analysts extract and communicate insights from existing data, while scientists create models and tools to predict or automate future outcomes.



The Short Answer: You are right to be concerned. "Data Scientist" is not dead, but it is depreciating. It is becoming a "squeeze" role—squeezed between "Data Analysts" (who are cheaper and use low-code tools) and "AI/ML Engineers" (who build production systems).
Here is the Red Team analysis of your current branding and a strategy for 2025.

?? Red Team Assessment: The Vulnerabilities
Your Thesis: "I am effectively a Data Scientist because I build models and automate tools, even though my title is Analyst." The Logic Failure: In 2025, the market does not award "credit" for work done above your pay grade unless you explicitly reframe it.
1. The "Analyst" Trap: If your official title is "Data Analyst," automated HR filters and recruiters will anchor you to a lower salary band (often 20-30% less than Scientists/Engineers). They will assume your "automation" is simple Excel macros or basic scripts, not robust software engineering.
2. The "Generalist" Risk: The title "Data Scientist" is fading because it has become too broad. It now often implies "someone who makes PowerPoints with more math." Companies are shifting budget toward Builders (Engineers) rather than Thinkers (Scientists), unless those Scientists have PhDs and are doing novel research.
3. The "Two-Job" Liability: You have a massive volume of output (WTCHP + DU), but if you brand it as "Analysis," you look like a high-throughput report generator. If you brand it as "Engineering," you look like a high-leverage architect. You are currently underselling your most valuable asset: automation.

?? The Pivot: "Smart Branding" for 2025/2026
You need to move your brand from "Insight Generation" (Analyst/Scientist) to "System Generation" (Engineer).
1. The "AI Engineer" (The Trend Play)
? Why: This is the highest-ROI title for 2025. It aligns perfectly with your interest in LLMs and Python. It signals that you don't just analyze data; you integrate AI into workflows to do the work for you.
? The Reframe: Your "automating tools" work is not "scripting"; it is "building Agentic workflows."
? Your New Tagline: "I build AI systems that automate complex decision-making."
2. The "Machine Learning Engineer" (The Safe Harbor)
? Why: This is the gold standard for non-PhD data professionals who code. It separates you from the "Excel crowd."
? The Reframe: Your "building models" work (like the WTCHP forecasting) is not "statistics"; it is "MLOps" (Machine Learning Operations).
? The Key Differentiator: Focus on the pipeline. Did you automate the data ingest? Did you automate the re-training? That is Engineering, not Science.
3. The "Applied Scientist" (The Precision Play)
? Why: This title is popular at big tech (Amazon, etc.) for people who sit exactly where you do: between the code and the math.
? The Nuance: It usually implies you can read a research paper and implement it in code. Given your "liberal arts/deep thinker" background, this fits your persona well, but it can sometimes hit a "PhD ceiling" in recruiting.


#186: Where to Work: So Many (Types of) Companies to Choose From!
1. Early Career Path Recommendation: The hosts generally recommend starting with consulting/agency work early in your career to gain broader experience across different industries and clients, then transitioning to in-house roles for deeper, more strategic work.
2. Pace and Pressure Differences: Agency work typically moves faster with stricter deadlines and less flexibility due to contractual obligations, while in-house roles offer more reasonable timelines and the ability to pivot projects based on emerging value.
3. Learning and Development Opportunities: Consulting provides rapid skill development across diverse projects (learning in one year what might take three years in-house), while in-house roles allow for deeper expertise and more successful project implementation since you can see initiatives through to completion.
4. Relationship Dynamics: In-house work offers stability in relationships and organizational knowledge, while consulting requires constantly building new relationships and understanding different business contexts, which can be both challenging and growth-promoting.
5. Focus on People and Culture Over Setting: The most important factor isn't whether you choose consulting or in-house work, but finding the right people, company culture, and learning opportunities - with many successful careers involving experience in both environments.
